                    <td>12/13/2017</td><td class="td-badge"><div class="fg badge">FG</div></td><td><a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/blogs/ian-kinsler-is-a-big-upgrade-for-a-minor-price/">Ian Kinsler Is a Big Upgrade for a Minor Price</a><span class="m_author"> by <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/blogs/index.php?author=7030">Jeff Sullivan</a></span></td></tr></table></div></td></tr>

        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>After a 2008 season that saw Kinsler go from above-average to legitimately awesome, 2009 has to be considered disappointing from a production standpoint. It was in that '08 season that Kinsler slashed .319/.375/.517, good for a near-.400 wOBA. That slash line dropped to .253/.327/.488 in 2009, thanks almost wholly to a decrease in BABIP from .339 to .245. That latter number is untenably low, but the change in BABIP also reflects a change in Kinsler's process. Consider: in 2008, Kinsler's line-drive and fly-ball rates were 24.2% and 43.3%, respectively. In 2009, those numbers changed to 15.9% and 54.0%. A higher fly-ball rate means more home runs, sure, but it almost always means a drop in BABIP, too. Kinsler's 2009 fly-ball rate placed him second (behind Carlos Pena) among 154 qualified hitters.<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>While Kinsler saw his batting average change greatly, his plate discipline and counting stats remain encouraging. His O-Swing% dropped slightly from his 2008 season, while his overall contact rate improved. Furthermore, his raw home-run, RBI, and stolen-base totals increased. Nor was all of that improvement due merely to the 23 more games he played in '09 than '08 (although the relative improvement in durability should not be discounted, either). Expect Kinsler's batting average to stabilize somewhere in the .270s, while both his home run and stolen base numbers find their way to the mid-20s again. However it plays out, Kinsler will almost certainly finish the season as top-five second baseman.  (Carson Cistulli)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Two leg injuries cost Ian Kinsler 69 days on the disabled list in 2010. That is worth remembering as it should hamper his projected playing-time totals. In addition to his loss of at-bats, last season brought a significant decrease in power production, perhaps tied to his injuries. After 31 home runs in 2009, Kinsler smacked just nine balls over the fence last season. Unsurprisingly, his stolen bases also suffered, down to 15 from 31 in 2009. Both should be expected to rebound to around the 20-25 level in 2011 but re-injury could risk Kinsler’s opportunity to accumulate those stats or might even force him to change his offensive strategy. That is the downside, though. His upside remains reachable -- that being a return to 2009’s home-run and stolen-base totals without the freakish low batting average that resulted from an unusual drop in batting average on balls in play. Kinsler is worth taking high, just make sure to have another second baseman that can cover for him if the power does not return or he lands on the disabled list again. (Matthew Carruth)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ian Kinsler is a good offensive second baseman, but coming off some troubling injuries. Nab him if healthy.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Coming into the season, the question with Kinsler was health. He'd been on the disabled list at least once every year from 2006-2010, but he managed to stay on the field in 2011 and had the best season of his career. He was one of only nine players to draw more walks (89) than strikeouts (71), and one of only four to go 30-30. Kinsler managed to be one of the very best fantasy second baseman despite his so-so average (.255) because he hit homers (32), stole bases (30), scored runs (121), and drove in some runs (77). Kinsler is still very much in the prime of his career at age 29, and he plays half his games in a great ballpark and all of them hitting atop a great lineup. It's a recipe for success, and now he has to build on his first full healthy season as a big leaguer to become one of the game's truly elite perennial fantasy producers. (Mike Axisa)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Kinsler avoided the disabled list and parlayed his health into the best season of his career in 2011. If he manages to stay on the field full-time next season, expect everything but a high average from the Rangers' second baseman.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>No one is exactly sure where Ian Kinsler will be playing on the diamond in 2013, but everyone knows that he’ll still be eligible at second base. Kinsler’s second base eligibility will drive his value upward, even if his counting stats remain at 2012 levels. Kinsler remains a reliable 20/20 threat even as he enters his age-31 season, but his runs and RBI are what have been pushing him above other second baseman on the rankings lists. It’s unlikely we’ll ever see a .285 batting average or a 30/30 season from Kinsler ever again, but he’ll still be one of the first second baseman selected on draft day. (Zach Sanders)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ian Kinsler is almost a yearly lock for a 20/20 season, but his runs scored and driven in are what elevates his value. Kinsler will be one of the first second baseman selected on draft day. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Whatever one makes of the Ian Kinsler - Prince Fielder trade between the Tigers and Rangers in real baseball, it is hard to deny that the shift in parks will hurt Kinsler's fantasy numbers a bit. Comerica is not the pitcher's paradise it is sometimes said to be, but it is definitely harder on hitters than the Ballpark in Arlington. Moreover, Kinsler is clearly on the decline -- he will be 32 in June, and the home run power and speed just are not what they where in 2009 a few years ago. That being said, it is hard to imagine a fantasy league in which Kinsler is not drafted. Kinsler is not on the same level as Robinson Cano, Matt Carpenter, or Dustin Pedroia anymore, but if those three players make up the top end of fantasy second basemen, Kinsler (at least for 2014) is still clearly right behind them. He has always popped up a lot, which hurts his batting average on balls in play and keeps his average from being great, but he has enough other skills to make up for it. His walk rate has been only average the last couple of years as opposed to the good walk rates of 2010 and 2011, but he also has excellent contact skills. His power and speed are not what they used to be, but 15-20 homers and roughly the same number of steals is still excellent from a second baseman. Don't treat him as a second base star, but do not let his declining numbers and tougher park let Kinsler slip too far. (Matt Klaassen)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ian Kinsler is not what he used to be, and his new park will be tougher on his numbers, but he still projects as a one of the better second-tier fantasy second basemen in almost all leagues.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Kinsler will probably do what he has done that last few seasons: hit around 15 homers; have an average between .250 and .275; steal 15 bases. He has a enough of a track record to estimate his 2015 value. Even with the consistent play, a couple of major dents are emerging. The first is a halving of his walk rate. It went from 8% to 4%, which corresponds to a drop in OBP from .344 to .307. Diving into his plate discipline stats, he is just swinging at more pitches than ever before. In 2013 he swung at 40% of all pitches. In 2014, that rate jumped to 47%. His value will be significantly different in average and OBP leagues. The second issue is his power. His home run per fly ball rate has gone from 12% to 6% while his average homer and fly ball distance has gone from 276 ft to 269 ft. Looks like there's less thunder in his bat these days. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Ian Kinsler has been a steady producer over the years and he should be that again in 2015. He has been showing some decline in his walk and home run rates recently, though.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Kinsler continued his pretty standard and slight decline in the counting categories in 2015, completely reasonable for a 33-year-old. What's more encouraging than him remaining a double digit guy in home runs and steals is that he turned in a terrific defensive season at second base, saving 19 runs by Defensive Runs Saved and 6.3 runs above average by Ultimate Zone Rating. That suggests to at least some degree that the underlying athleticism that's made Kinsler a fixture on the top five list at the position for a decade still remains. At the dish, he picked things up as the season went on, doing a better job putting the ball in the air and hitting the ball much harder. His July and August were remarkable, and even if he cooled some, he showed last year that he still owns the skills that have long made him a stud at the keystone. With a low strikeout rate, Kinsler's fly balls don't present too much of a problem, and he figures to hit around .275. If the five percent home run per fly ball rate can rebound to even a pedestrian number and he stays healthy once again -- his durability hasn't really been a problem since 2010 -- he'll have serious five-category juice. Given that the position has a lot of question marks and Kinsler really isn't one, he's pretty safely a top 10 second bagger once again. (Blake Murphy)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Another year, another good two-way season for Kinsler. He's slowly moved from a 30-30 threat to a 20-20 to maybe a 12-12, but he remains reliable even despite a modest home run rate.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>So, that came out of nowhere. Kinsler, who hadn't eclipsed 20 home runs since 2011, suddenly threatened the 30-homer mark in 2016. He hit 23% better than the league, tied for his second-best season of his career, yet he finished only seventh among second basemen per ESPN's Player Rater, which attests to the keystone's newfound depth. One could say Kinsler sold out for his power - the homer spike coincided with a career-high strikeout rate fueled by his worst contact rate on out-of-zone pitches. It's just hard to tell if it's legitimate or sustainable, what with the league-wide power surge and all. His peripherals support the bump, which is nice, but it's hard to believe he'll maintain a home run-to-fly ball rate almost double what it was from the previous four years. He's entering his age-35 season, so it would be unwise, although not absurd, to expect a repeat of last season. Something closer to his 15-15-.270ish season in 2013 seems most reasonable  — Steamer expects as much, but takes the under on the stolen bases — but it makes him more of a back-end option in standard leagues. As long as he's not too expensive, you can at least buy the vintage, reliable Kinsler and dream on the upside of the possibility of another 25 homers. (Alex Chamberlain)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Kinsler's surprising power spike coincided with the league-wide surge, making it a bit hard to trust. It's wiser to draft him expecting modest contributions to all five categories and not much more, although you can hold out on the dream of a 2016 repeat. He's a back-end second baseman in standard mixed leagues.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

With a low strikeout rate, Kinsler's fly balls don't present too much of a problem, and he figures to hit around .275. If the five percent home run per fly ball rate can rebound to even a pedestrian number and he stays healthy once again -- his durability hasn't really been a problem since 2010 -- he'll have serious five-category juice. Given that the position has a lot of question marks and Kinsler really isn't one, he's pretty safely a top 10 second bagger once again. (Blake Murphy)",

One could say Kinsler sold out for his power - the homer spike coincided with a career-high strikeout rate fueled by his worst contact rate on out-of-zone pitches. It's just hard to tell if it's legitimate or sustainable, what with the league-wide power surge and all. His peripherals support the bump, which is nice, but it's hard to believe he'll maintain a home run-to-fly ball rate almost double what it was from the previous four years. He's entering his age-35 season, so it would be unwise, although not absurd, to expect a repeat of last season. Something closer to his 15-15-.270ish season in 2013 seems most reasonable  — Steamer expects as much, but takes the under on the stolen bases — but it makes him more of a back-end option in standard leagues.
